# 298\n
{Edinburgh, Royal College of Physicians, MS of Cursor Mundi, hand B, fols. 16r-36v: Extracts from the Northern Homily Collection}\n
C14a\n
455 476 N\n
{=NRY=}\n
{=Sample represents all the English in this hand=}\n
{=Script - small neat Textura=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=The extracts from the Northern Homily Collection are sandwiched\n
between two imperfect and wrongly ordered sections of Cursor Mundi\n
in Hands A and C. The only edition is the 1862 one by John Small who\n
expands abbreviations silently and normalises much of the spelling.\n
The changes are so extensive I have made no attempt to note their\n
occurrences. The scribe\'s thorn is open-topped but different in\n
shape from <y>, which is also used beside <i> for both [i] and [j], unlike\n
Scribe C\'s habit. Punctuation is virtually absent except at the\n
beginning where the space left for the enlarged capital throws\n
the lineation out for four lines and punctus are therefore\n
employed to indicate verse line ends. Punctus on either side\n
of capital <I> are taken to be part of the figura and are\n
ignored. Forms of majuscule and minuscule thorn, <w> and <v> are\n
identical in this hand. The majuscule forms of all other letters\n
are regularly used at the start of each verse line. I have followed\n
Small in indicating capitals in this position for thorn <w> and <v>\n
also. Abbreviations are usually standard and expanded in the normal way.\n
As with Hand C, the same abbreviation as for <er> (a hook over the preceding\n
letter is also sometimes used on stem-final double <l> where <er> would\n
not be a suitable expansion. Here I have transcribed the abbreviation as\n
~, e.g. TELL~ for "tells". Another abbreviation for <er> is a broken\n
vertical line above where the letters are missing. This is also used\n
for <re> and is expanded in these cases according to context. Spaces\n
have been left for two-line initial capitals not carried out. Visible\n
guide letters supply the lack as noted. Sectional paraphs may also\n
have been left for the rubricator to fill in. Hairline double hyphens,\n
as noted, serve as paraphs=}\n
{~f16ra~}\n
$father/n-voc_>*F>*ADER\n
{=Space has been left for a 3-line illuminated capital which has not\n
been carried out. *F is in the form of a guide letter (doubled\n
minuscule F) in the left side of the empty box=}\n

$pre:onan/vps13_Pr^INN+ES $/vps13[N]_+ES\n
{="pierces?, injures?". Small (1862) prints PINNES but the superscript I\n
as abbreviation for RI is clear. See MED pre:nen v., especially in relation\n
to MED priken v. sense 1 (c). The senses of priken and pre:nen cover both\n
"pierce" and "pin together" in both literal and figurative senses. Cf. also\n
MED prinnen v. which I believe should be listed rather under pre:nen with\n
the sense "secure"=}\n

$arraign/vn<prF_REIN+IGE $/vn<prF_+IGE\n
{=Small (1862) reads ROUNGE, see MED roungen v. which means "chew on, gnaw,\n
consume". MED cites this reading under sense 1 (c) and suggests "be\n
tormented" which stretches the sense a great deal. I am confident\n
that Small\'s reading is not right not least because an infinitive is\n
not what the syntax demands here. The second letter is almost\n
certainly a badly formed E with an enlarged bow making it seem\n
O-like. This scribe quite frequently forms E in this way. I believe\n
the correct reading to be REINIGE. A verbal noun makes more sense\n
syntactically and this scribe seems always to have -IGE ending\n
for verbal nouns if the verb\'s stem ends in N. He rarely defines\n
I with a hairline stroke outwith minim contexts and does not always\n
do so even in minim contexts so lack of hairlines is no bar to this\n
reading. Semantically it is far superior; see MED reinen v. (3) and\n
cf. arreinen v. both used in the context of the Day of Judgment and\n
both meaning "arraign, try". The verbal noun is not cited but would\n
mean "trial, judgment". This reading if accepted would be an\n
ante-dating of the verb in English=}\n

$as/pr_ALS\n
{=Here, and elsewhere as marked, ALS seems to have the function of a\n
preposition with the temporal sense "on". It appears to be a northern\n
usage though I can find no mention of it in OED or MED. It is listed in\n
DOST s.v. As, adv. and conj. sense 4 and is taken to be a reduced form\n
of "as of, as to" meaning "in regards, in respect or consideration of".\n
See the quotations under (3): Darius...echapyd as that day; Wynt IV.\n
660. As this tid.. with him he wald nocht bide; Wall. I. 313. KW (pers.\n
comm.) reports many instances of this prepositional use of "as" in the\n
Older Scots documents.  A possible mechanism for this usage to have\n
arisen might be as follows: (1) "as" used in combination with preposition,\n
perhaps most commonly in legal phraseology, e.g. "as on this day",\n
"as at this time"; (2) possible simplifications of this usage could\n
involve: either omitting "as", reverting to the more common, simple\n
prepositional phrase, "on this day", "at this time"; or omitting the\n
original preposition and letting the "as" substitute for it. The latter\n
seems to be what has happened here. Because the phrase seems formally\n
to require a preposition I have tagged "as" accordingly=}\n

lobe of the D with the following O makes it most likely to have\n
been intended (whether mistakenly or not) as a D=}\n

{\\}\n
!_*ROB\n
{=The C version of this text has "but". Here the *R and the B are quite\n
clear. The vowel is probably O but could just possibly be read as E.\n
Small\'s (1862) suggestion that it is for ROV "cloth" from OE "reov"\n
(as he puts it) is highly unlikely. OE re:af is normally "garment,\n
vestment" and would not be found in a candle. I do not know what\n
the word can be, but the context requires that it mean the material\n
from which a candlewick is made. MED s.v. ribbe n. 2 cites spellings\n
in reb-, rob- and rub- as well as rib- but there is no evidence that\n
"ribwort" or "plantain" (the meanings given) have fibres suitable for\n
candlewicks, unless "ribbe" could also be used for flax or tow. Other\n
possibilities are that *ROB is written for "rope" (but in this\n
language we would expect RAP(E)) or that it is an error for "reed"\n
which also seems far-fetched=}\n
$wick/n_WEC\n
